  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }
    
    function initAppleId() {
      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',
        redirectURI: 'https://www.goodreads.com/apple_users/sign_in_with_apple_web',
        state: 'apple_oauth_state_949f0976-fee9-415d-931a-04b72b8560c8'
      });
    }

      var APPLE_SIGN_IN_JS_URL =  "https://appleid.cdn-apple.com/appleauth/static/jsapi/appleid/1/en_US/appleid.auth.js"

    <a title="Paul Bryant" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/416390-paul-bryant"><img alt="Paul Bryant" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1224113172p2/416390.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/961174065?book_show_action=true">Jun 08, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Paul Bryant" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Paul Bryant" href="/user/show/416390-paul-bryant">Paul Bryant</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="did not like it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">did not like it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">

              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/416390-paul-bryant?shelf=novels">novels</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer961174065" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10710645354606204995">This was exactly the novel I didn’t want to read, but at least it’s official now – NO MORE IAN MCEWAN BOOKS FOR ME, EVER. I would like to tell you how stupid this novel is, but Maciek beat me to it – see his great review here<br/><br/><a target='_blank' rel='noopener nofollow' href='https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/145831867'>https://www.goodreads.com/review/show...</a><br/><br/>So let me tell you about the boring sentences you have to accept into your life if you read this book. Here’s one:<br/><br/><i>Now, in the late afternoon, although the sun was still high, the eastern sky had lost its vivid purple and, fading by deg</i></span>
  <span id="freeText10710645354606204995" style="display:none">This was exactly the novel I didn’t want to read, but at least it’s official now – NO MORE IAN MCEWAN BOOKS FOR ME, EVER. I would like to tell you how stupid this novel is, but Maciek beat me to it – see his great review here<br /><br /><a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/145831867">https://www.goodreads.com/review/show...</a><br /><br />So let me tell you about the boring sentences you have to accept into your life if you read this book. Here’s one:<br /><br /><i>Now, in the late afternoon, although the sun was still high, the eastern sky had lost its vivid purple and, fading by degrees through nursery blue to diluted milk, effected, across the precise line of the horizon, the most delicate of transactions with the pale grey of the sea.</i><br /><br />This is, I take it, what people mean when they harp on about the Ian McEwan prose style. It looks, to me, as if, Mr McEwan, has broken in to James Ellroy’s office, and, stolen all James’ commas. Never have, I seen, so many clauses, and commas, in one short, novel. For me the effect is akin to reading through a stocking mask, the kind that robbers used before they all switched to balaclavas. Especially when our prose stylist is continually, dementedly, describing the weather, the streets of Venice, or the furniture in the rooms. Ah how he loves furniture. Cutlery too. <br /><br />You can tell this is pretentious I mean literary because although it’s set in Venice the V word is never mentioned. <br /><br />By now I have realised what Ian McEwan’s USP is. What he does is he describes in tedious detail a couple of ordinary novocained middle class English types in an ordinary situation and just when you’re dozing off he has a page of lurid violence. Sometimes the lurid violence comes at the beginning, sometimes in the middle, and here at the end. <br /><br />Here’s another McEwanbite for you. I think this is the way dead people would write, if they could :<br /><br /><i>In the evening they decided they were suffering from lack of exercise and made plans to catch the boat across the lagoon the next day to the popular strip of land whose beaches faced the open sea. This led them to talk at length and euphorically, for they had just smoked another joint, about swimming, their preferred strokes, the relative merits of rivers, lakes, swimming-pools and seas, and the precise nature of the attraction water had for people : was it the buried memory of ancient sea ancestors? Talk of memory caused Mary to frown again. The conversation became desultory after that, and they went to bed earlier than usual, a little before midnight.</i><br /><br />Notice “the popular strip of land whose beaches faced the open sea” – he can’t give it its name, which would be the natural thing to do, because for some reason of high literature, he has decided not to say that the city with its canals and no traffic is Venice. So he has to use this forced circumlocution.<br /><br />This novel means nothing. It portentously gestures towards some kind of statement critical of men who think that women really like to be beaten up and by extension how feminism is destroying life as we know it but the denouement capsizes any attempt to make sense of the plot.<br /><br />This novel promotes yet another version of the concept that (some) victims actively participate in their own destruction. Why do they do this? Well, who knows, not Ian McEwan, that’s for sure. They just do. Too much novocaine maybe. <br /><br />I am promoting the idea that readers can do without Ian McEwan. </span>
  <a data-text-id="10710645354606204995" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1844541605" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1844541605" />
    <a title="Lisa" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/32532774-lisa"><img alt="Lisa" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1480492067p2/32532774.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1844541605?book_show_action=true">Dec 20, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Lisa" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Lisa" href="/user/show/32532774-lisa">Lisa</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/32532774-lisa?shelf=1001-books-to-read-before-you-die">1001-books-to-read-before-you-die</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/32532774-lisa?shelf=don-t-read-it">don-t-read-it</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/32532774-lisa?shelf=forgettable">forgettable</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/32532774-lisa?shelf=monster-mash-of-a-mess">monster-mash-of-a-mess</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1844541605" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17621166358436381070">Introduction:<br/><br/>As I have taught teenagers the art of writing an essay, and they have taught me the art of detecting a shortcut, I will apply it here (the shortcut, that is), for honestly, the deeply hidden teenager in me tells me that I should not, under any circumstances, waste too much time writing a review of this book. To search for a reasonable plot, characterisation, or purpose (except for cheap voyeurism and violence) is quite futile. I should, however, mark it as read and forgettable befor</span>
  <span id="freeText17621166358436381070" style="display:none">Introduction:<br /><br />As I have taught teenagers the art of writing an essay, and they have taught me the art of detecting a shortcut, I will apply it here (the shortcut, that is), for honestly, the deeply hidden teenager in me tells me that I should not, under any circumstances, waste too much time writing a review of this book. To search for a reasonable plot, characterisation, or purpose (except for cheap voyeurism and violence) is quite futile. I should, however, mark it as read and forgettable before I forget it again. So here is the review, with quotes and all:<br /><br />(Teacher’s note and feedback: "Do not speak of yourself and your experience in the introduction, remain neutral!" Student’s reaction: “I am not neutral!”)<br /><br />Main Body: <br /><br />(Teacher’s note and feedback: "Do not call the main body of your essay main body, come up with something more telling!" Student’s change: “The Novel”) <br /><br />For my essay, I will use two significant quotes from the novel and paraphrase them to match my reading experience. (Teacher’s note: "Try to find a more subtle transition from introduction to main part!" Student’s question: "Why? And how?")<br /><br />Quote 1 from the text:<br /><br />“She sleepwalked from moment to moment, and whole months slipped by without memory, without bearing the faintest imprint of her conscious will. ” <br /><br />Paraphrase to explain reader’s experience:<br /><br />“She sleepwalked from page to page, and whole chapters slipped by without memory, without bearing the faintest imprint of her conscious will. ” <br /><br />(Teacher’s note: "Can you explain that more in detail, please?" Student: "No, I have forgotten the rest!")<br /><br />Second reference from the text, as required in the rubric:<br /><br />“What tended to happen, to Colin and Mary at least, was that subjects were not explored so much as defensively reiterated, or forced into elaborate irrelevancies, and suffused with irritability.” <br /><br />Paraphrase:<br /><br />“What tended to happen, reading McEwan at least, was that subjects were not explored so much as defensively reiterated, or forced into elaborate irrelevancies, and suffused with irritability.” <br /><br />Conclusion:<br /><br />I think this student has studied Ian McEwan enough to make the decision to give it up entirely for the time being, and to explore other realms of literature from now on. There is not much to hope for! His novels are a mixed bag, some are tolerably good, others are interesting at least, and some are just plain forgettable. The last two or three I read were of the forgettable kind, and there is just too much good stuff to read out there to repeatedly engage in “reiterations of elaborate irrelevancies”. Ten or eleven of Ian McEwan’s novels are more than enough!<br /><br />Note from student to teacher: "I fulfilled the rubric, so you will have to give me the marks. And it is okay to present your own opinion in the conclusion. You said so yourself!"<br /><br />Note from teacher: "Be careful to consistently use formal language. Grade and individual, detailed feedback will be returned after Christmas break!"<br /><br />Student: "Oh, come on!"<br /><br />Teacher: "I am a human being as well, and need my weekends!"<br /><br />Student: "Unreliable statement, lacking evidence!"<br /><br />Teacher: "These kids nowadays leave me speechless… but they are more entertaining than the novel I just read! I wonder if my Student Alter Ego even read it?"<br /><br />Student: "No, Teacher Alter Ego, I did not. For if I had, I would have told you that it is not for students: gratuitous violence, unmotivated, random story lines, superficially shocking…"<br /><br />Teacher Alter Ego: "Fair enough, is would not have been possible to make a proper characterisation or plot analysis either, as the protagonists are completely unrealistic: who would follow a stranger to his house and listen to his stupid life story and then return despite clear indications that he is brutal and manipulative…"<br /><br />Student Alter Ego: "My point precisely. I stopped listening to him and went home before it got creepy!"<br /><br />Teacher Alter Ego: "Good, then we don’t have to add spoilers. How are we going to rate it?"<br /><br />SAE: "The book I haven’t read, you mean?"<br /><br />TAE: "But I have, unfortunately, - side effect of the profession. So?"<br /><br />SAE: "Give it 2 stars, then. It is not as bad as <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/865.The_Alchemist" title="The Alchemist by Paulo Coelho" rel="nofollow noopener">The Alchemist</a> or <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/968.The_Da_Vinci_Code__Robert_Langdon___2_" title="The Da Vinci Code (Robert Langdon, #2) by Dan Brown" rel="nofollow noopener">The Da Vinci Code</a>."<br /><br />TAE: "Good point. 2 stars. But no recommendation."</span>
  <a data-text-id="17621166358436381070" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Orsodimondo" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Orsodimondo" href="/user/show/10490224-orsodimondo">Orsodimondo</a>

        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9788806561505" href="/book/show/12453154-cortesie-per-gli-ospiti">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/10490224-orsodimondo?shelf=inglese">inglese</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3460660961" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1077925768145378380"><b>IAN MACABRE</b><br/><br/><img src='https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1595654644i/29861004._SX540_.jpg' class='gr-hostedUserImg'/><br/><b>Christopher Walken è Robert, Natasha Richardson è Mary, Rupert Everett è Colin nel film omonimo di Paul Schrader del 1990.</b><br/><br/><i>Abitavamo due mondi<br/>le figlie e le madri<br/>nel regno dei figli.</i><br/>L’epigrafe della poetessa americana Adrienne Rich.<br/><br/>“Ian Macabre” all’ennesima potenza, qui, più che mai, il soprannome di McEwan mi sembra azzeccato.<br/><br/><img src='https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1595654644i/29861005._SX540_.jpg' class='gr-hostedUserImg'/><br/><b>I quattro i protagonisti, inclusa Helen Mirren nel ruolo di Caroline, la moglie di Robert.</b><br/><br/>In una città sull’acqua, mai nominata ma nella quale è facile riconosc</span>
  <span id="freeText1077925768145378380" style="display:none"><b>IAN MACABRE</b><br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1595654644i/29861004._SX540_.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><b>Christopher Walken è Robert, Natasha Richardson è Mary, Rupert Everett è Colin nel film omonimo di Paul Schrader del 1990.</b><br><br><i>Abitavamo due mondi<br>le figlie e le madri<br>nel regno dei figli.</i><br>L’epigrafe della poetessa americana Adrienne Rich.<br><br>“Ian Macabre” all’ennesima potenza, qui, più che mai, il soprannome di McEwan mi sembra azzeccato.<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1595654644i/29861005._SX540_.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><b>I quattro i protagonisti, inclusa Helen Mirren nel ruolo di Caroline, la moglie di Robert.</b><br><br>In una città sull’acqua, mai nominata ma nella quale è facile riconoscere Venezia, una coppia di turisti inglesi, Colin e Mary, trascorre giornate consultando la cartina e continuando a perdere la strada, cercando l’ombra per ripararsi da un caldo che toglie le forze, dormendo nella stessa stanza d’albergo, ma in letti separati pur ‘consumando’ sesso insieme, sesso che appare una specie di rituale residuo di sette anni di convivenza e poco ha a che spartire col desiderio e la passione.<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1595654644i/29861006.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>Una sera, smarrita la strada come di consueto, incontrano Robert, misterioso e affascinante abitante della città. Lo incontrano per caso o Robert ‘facilita’ l’incontro?<br>Robert si prende subito cura dei due giovani, li porta nel suo splendido palazzo, li rifocilla, li introduce alla moglie Caroline, che appare sottomessa al marito.<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1595654644i/29861007._SX540_.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>La mattina dopo Colin e Mary si risvegliano nudi in una stanza da letto della magione di Robert e Caroline. Da questo momento il sesso tra loro comincia a fare scintille, hanno ritrovato slancio e linfa vitale. E da questo momento sono oggetto delle cure e attenzioni di Robert e Caroline, ma soprattutto del primo, diventano gli ospiti da riempire di cortesie, ma dai quali anche attendere conforto e consolazione, come il titolo originale lascia intuire (The Comfort of Strangers).<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1595654644i/29861008._SX540_.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>Robert racconta ai due ospiti del padre dispotico e autoritario che ha avuto, lui, figlio minore e unico maschio. Robert lancia, o semina, indizi che costruiscono inquietudine. Cos’è che può succedere, cos’è che accadrà? C’è per caso un’attrazione omosessuale tra lui e Colin, o è piuttosto un rapporto tra due maschi di età diversa che ricorda quello tra un padre e suo figlio?<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1595654644i/29861009._SX540_.png" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>Un po’ gotico, un po’ à la Morte a Venezia, tra decadenza e sentore di pericolo McEwan gioca col suo consueto sadismo e la perversione a tenere inchiodata l’attenzione del lettore. Ma non aspettiamoci colpi di scena: non prima del gran finale. McEwan sparge indizi, lavorando sui particolari con una minuzia descrittiva che evoca una cura visiva molto stimolante per il cinema.<br>Robert e Caroline, come ragni esperti, imbastiscono la loro tela, aspettando che si presentino le prede giuste.<br>E poi, ecco, <i>improvvisamente fu lungo il doppio</i>, zac, la crudeltà è servita.<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1595654644i/29861010.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1595654644i/29861011._SY540_.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"></span>
  <a data-text-id="1077925768145378380" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<div id="review_145831867" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/145831867" />
    <a title="Maciek" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2969647-maciek"><img alt="Maciek" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1280839159p2/2969647.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/145831867?book_show_action=true">Feb 04, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Maciek" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Maciek" href="/user/show/2969647-maciek">Maciek</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780099754916" href="/book/show/246543.The_Comfort_of_Strangers">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2969647-maciek?shelf=read-in-2011">read-in-2011</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2969647-maciek?shelf=reviewed">reviewed</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2969647-maciek?shelf=booker-long-and-shortlist">booker-long-and-shortlist</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer145831867" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16908913668056032196">This was the first novel by Ian McEwan that I've read ages ago. I got a copy from a supermarket sale and read it in a day, wondering all the time how someone could write down so many nasty things and get away with it. Cut me some slack, I was 10.<br/><br/>So, as I sat to rediscover this classic, I hoped to feel at least a fraction of the weird pleasure this novel brought me back then. The thing is short - 120 pages long - and you can easily breeze through it in two hours.<br/><br/>I have to say that this might be M</span>
  <span id="freeText16908913668056032196" style="display:none">This was the first novel by Ian McEwan that I've read ages ago. I got a copy from a supermarket sale and read it in a day, wondering all the time how someone could write down so many nasty things and get away with it. Cut me some slack, I was 10.<br /><br />So, as I sat to rediscover this classic, I hoped to feel at least a fraction of the weird pleasure this novel brought me back then. The thing is short - 120 pages long - and you can easily breeze through it in two hours.<br /><br />I have to say that this might be McEwan's darkest book, but it's also his most nonsensical, beating even the infamous <i>On Chesil Beach</i>. NOTHING in this book makes any sort of sense whatsoever, and the plot can be best described as "two morons go on a holiday where they meet two crazy people who do horrible things to them for no apparent reason". McEwan can write and the best thing about this novel is his prose, but unfortunately he proves that he can write about nothing in particular and not only get away with it but also win accolades for his fiction.<br /><br />Amazingly impropable plot developments (the couple goes to the beach when the sky is completely overcast, and is even described as being black...the bars are closed at 9PM in a big city(!) and when the couple find a bar, the waiter refuses to serve them water(!!)) combined with amazingly stupid and inconsistent character actions (the man our couple meets is a thug with a gold chain, who shares on the spot the story how his father abused his siters and how they took revenge of thim, which included a lot of feces and turkish rugs - AND THEY GO AWAY WITH THIS DUDE TO HIS HOUSE, it's true check for yourself!) combined with a lot of calculated, creepy stuff thrown in for further shock value (the man tells the girl that he'd like to bind her to a machine that would constnatly penetrate her body, even after her death - and she tells him that she'd like to cut his limbs off and keep him for sex - AND THESE PEOPLE HAVE CHILDREN, thank God this is fiction!) combined with a lot of schtooping and booze (the two lovers eat practically nothing, sleep in the streets(!), drink gallons of alcohol and when they get back to a hotel they make sweet and passionate love for like FOUR DAYS - what was McEwan thinking?).<br /><br />All of this is written in McEwan's cool, detached prose, with not a smile in sight, which leads me to the interpretation that he was writing this as a satire of some sorts, a black comedy, a spoof, daring the reader to laugh out loud. But the back blurb speaks about "an elegant and miniature gothic...smooth and classy", and McEwan himself stares at me with his confident, serious look from the author portrait, which means that both he and the readers actually took this stuff seriously. How they could is beyond me. Is everyone stupid? Maybe the paragraph where the man reveals the fact that he feels an emptiness between his scrotum and anus was to be taken as "the power of evil"? Or when they find themselves naked in the house of the guy they've meet (the feces guy) and accept his dinner proposition is to be taken as "erotic menace"? I don't known. Stupidier things happened to people, but nowhere you'll find a cumulation of them more embarassingly put together than in <i>The Comfort of Strangers</i>.</span>
  <a data-text-id="16908913668056032196" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2354255141" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2354255141" />
    <a title="Glenn Sumi" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/28854579-glenn-sumi"><img alt="Glenn Sumi" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1444333567p2/28854579.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2354255141?book_show_action=true">Apr 11, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Glenn Sumi" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Glenn Sumi" href="/user/show/28854579-glenn-sumi">Glenn Sumi</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 0099754916" href="/book/show/11935587-the-comfort-of-strangers">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2354255141" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3209913061755348638"><i>
  <b>Yikes. Did I just read that? Or did I dream it?</b>
</i><br/>- thoughts just after finishing <i>The Comfort Of Strangers</i><br/><br/>3.5 rounded up to 4. <br/><br/>After reading three Ian McEwan novels, each one different but all rather elegant and civilized, I thought I’d try one of his early books, written back when he had the nickname “Ian Macabre.”<br/><br/><i>The Comfort Of Strangers</i> (1981) is a chilling psychological thriller about Colin and Mary, an attractive, if rather self-absorbed, unmarried couple who are vacationing in an unnamed </span>
  <span id="freeText3209913061755348638" style="display:none"><i>
  <b>Yikes. Did I just read that? Or did I dream it?</b>
</i><br />- thoughts just after finishing <i>The Comfort Of Strangers</i><br /><br />3.5 rounded up to 4. <br /><br />After reading three Ian McEwan novels, each one different but all rather elegant and civilized, I thought I’d try one of his early books, written back when he had the nickname “Ian Macabre.”<br /><br /><i>The Comfort Of Strangers</i> (1981) is a chilling psychological thriller about Colin and Mary, an attractive, if rather self-absorbed, unmarried couple who are vacationing in an unnamed city that has all the old-world markers of Venice. One night, while looking for a restaurant, they get lost in the narrow, labyrinthine alleys and run into Robert, a gruff older man who grasps them a little too tightly and leads them to a bar. <br /><br />After a long night at the bar (which it turns out Robert owns) and a surreal early morning spent wandering around in a daze, they once again run into the man. Coincidence? Creepy? Whatever. Robert appears friendly and invites them to his spacious home, which he shares with his nervous wife, Caroline, who’s got a mysterious disability. <br /><br />I won’t reveal what happens next, but I will say that you’ll be reading between the lines, questioning the older couple’s motives and the younger couple’s naivete. A weird whiff of sexual and erotic menace hovers over much of the second half of the book; at one point the tourists wake up naked in a strange bed and Colin’s forced to either wear a woman’s nightie or a towel. <br /><br />This book threw me off-balance. Several times, especially from the midway point onwards, it felt like the narrative ground beneath me was shifting. It didn’t help that Colin and Mary seemed to have no one they could talk to. It made me wonder whether this was an allegory about relationships and alienation. And you might want to consider the title of another famous novella set in this same city. (Hint: it’s written by a Nobel-winning German author.)<br /><br />I’m not convinced this was the best use of McEwan’s talents. (Of course he would go on to much greater acclaim a few years later.) But there are a couple of sequences - including a long, extended story that Robert tells about his father, and the horrific climax, which we’ve been dreading for chapters - that are simply transfixing. <br /><br />I’ve only been to Venice once, but if I ever return, this sinister book will be rattling around in my head if I ever find myself lost and trying to get back to my hotel. <i>Mio dio</i> I hope that never happens.</span>
  <a data-text-id="3209913061755348638" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_76452356" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/76452356" />
    <a title="Agnieszka" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2446508-agnieszka"><img alt="Agnieszka" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1371835955p2/2446508.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/76452356?book_show_action=true">Nov 02, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Agnieszka" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Agnieszka" href="/user/show/2446508-agnieszka">Agnieszka</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780099754916" href="/book/show/246543.The_Comfort_of_Strangers">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2446508-agnieszka?shelf=mcewan">mcewan</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2446508-agnieszka?shelf=reviewed">reviewed</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2446508-agnieszka?shelf=disturbing">disturbing</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer76452356" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17080513802109753006"><br/>I normally don't write negative reviews. But this novel far too long bothered me so I need to get out it of my system. I hope it helps.<br/><br/>Definitely the worst McEwan’s novel I’ve read by now. It’s creepy and stupid. I mean protagonists are stupid and some creepy things happen to them. Probably because they are stupid. Colin and Mary go for a holiday to take some rest, escape ennui and maybe rekindle withered desire. Since I’m generous today I imagine for them Venice, though I don't remember if the </span>
  <span id="freeText17080513802109753006" style="display:none"><br />I normally don't write negative reviews. But this novel far too long bothered me so I need to get out it of my system. I hope it helps.<br /><br />Definitely the worst McEwan’s novel I’ve read by now. It’s creepy and stupid. I mean protagonists are stupid and some creepy things happen to them. Probably because they are stupid. Colin and Mary go for a holiday to take some rest, escape ennui and maybe rekindle withered desire. Since I’m generous today I imagine for them Venice, though I don't remember if the name of the city was even mentioned. Anyway, on the spot they’re wandering, drinking a lot, making love. Typical holiday stuff. <br /><br />During their tours through the city, they're constantly losing their way what probably had to herald impending disaster, they meet a mysterious man, Robert, who offers them his services as a guide . Charismatic and persuasive stranger ( is he really that one or maybe our dorks, to crown it all, are blind ones? ) invites them to his residence where they will meet his physically disabled wife, Caroline. And instead of take to their heels, one could see there was something wrong coming a mile off, our lovers accept invitation. It's hard to perceive every stranger as a pervert or psychopath with an axe but a bit common sense didn’t do harm to anyone yet. But, well if stupidity had wings these two would fly as (turtle)doves.<br /><br /><i> The comfort of strangers </i> is one of the earliest McEwan’s works but we already can discern here typical to his writing obsessions that would become his hallmark. Dash of macabre, menace, quirk, meanness and surreal aura; though, to his credit, in his later works these ingredients are much better balanced. This one was promoted as mixture of thriller and study of dark sides of human psyche. Well, unfortunately to me it was unreliable, repulsive and unimaginable display of stupidity and cruelty. I found it calculating, with the aim of shocking a reader ( well done, Mr McEwan ) and depressing as fuck. But written as usual with impeccable, somewhat impersonal and cold prose. Thus two stars.<br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="17080513802109753006" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1517491551?book_show_action=true">Jan 19, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="❀Julie" class="user" itemprop="url" name="❀Julie" href="/user/show/7425446-julie">❀Julie</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780140112832" href="/book/show/1366101.The_Comfort_of_Strangers">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1517491551" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1260198733476588384">I seem to be in the minority based on the ratings, but something about this story grabbed me right from the start.  There is irony in the title as the story brings anything <i>but</i> comfort, which may actually be why I liked it.  The characters are rather strange and unlikable, their relationships are strained, and the story reaffirms everything you were taught as a child about <i>not</i> talking to strangers.  An English couple’s holiday takes on an eerie twist when they find themselves lured in by a stran</span>
  <span id="freeText1260198733476588384" style="display:none">I seem to be in the minority based on the ratings, but something about this story grabbed me right from the start.  There is irony in the title as the story brings anything <i>but</i> comfort, which may actually be why I liked it.  The characters are rather strange and unlikable, their relationships are strained, and the story reaffirms everything you were taught as a child about <i>not</i> talking to strangers.  An English couple’s holiday takes on an eerie twist when they find themselves lured in by a stranger after becoming lost.  What follows is twisted and disturbing, but had me completely mesmerized, and having read some of Ian McEwan’s other books I knew he had a tendency to take the story into some bizarre territory.  The ending was very creepy but I think it was the sinister tone and slowly built tension that will stay with me.  Four stars for the thrill of the suspense and not being able to put it down.  Thanks to my local used bookstore for recommending this book!</span>

          </span>

<div id="review_1045455197" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1045455197" />
    <a title="Fabian" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1036893-fabian"><img alt="Fabian" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1514061293p2/1036893.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1045455197?book_show_action=true">Sep 04, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Fabian" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Fabian" href="/user/show/1036893-fabian">Fabian</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1045455197" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17541601011567455382">"The Comfort of Strangers" is the unfortunate combination of two of McEwan's worst books (which were published decades later, obviously): "Saturday" & "On Chesil Beach." It has that PVoAA (Powerful Voice of Authentic Authority), or that I've-published-stuff-now-I-will-see-places-with-the-eyes-of-a-wealthy-man tone. Ya know, the rich who think they're above even common violence (made, obviously, more "horrific" by their occurrence to a man of abundant means, gag). It also has that cringe-worthy v</span>
  <span id="freeText17541601011567455382" style="display:none">"The Comfort of Strangers" is the unfortunate combination of two of McEwan's worst books (which were published decades later, obviously): "Saturday" &amp; "On Chesil Beach." It has that PVoAA (Powerful Voice of Authentic Authority), or that I've-published-stuff-now-I-will-see-places-with-the-eyes-of-a-wealthy-man tone. Ya know, the rich who think they're above even common violence (made, obviously, more "horrific" by their occurrence to a man of abundant means, gag). It also has that cringe-worthy vanillaness, exemplified rather well in "On Chesil Beach." There are also two lovers in this one; &amp;, like on "Chesil Beach", you couldn't give a rat's ass about either one of 'em.<br /><br />McEwan's description, impeccable obviously, quickly becomes a banal thing (and before you pooh-pooh it, think also that at a scant 127 pages, the dude shoulda, minimal, CAPTIVATED us). It's as if McEwan lost track of his plot (we get, we get it: the randomness of events that occur to the average [and I must emphasize AVERAGE] tourist, means basically a looser script, a venture toward a certain plotlessness...) and lashing out in strong but overdoingit strokes, his belting-out of description description DESCRIPTION establishes an ambiance that hinders the main characters themselves. Our dislike is a quick but lasting thing. (Like, imagine for a sec. that Virgil was a dullard in the "Inferno"-- Dante's enterprise would've collapsed! There are certain entities that take our hands to take us places, and I believe it is intolerable to make them dunces, dullards, or pure-Fillers.)<br /><br />Like, who cares? shall be the overwhelming and haunted thought reoccuring in your brain throughout as you try to figure out why this book is anywhere near the Great Works at all. (Is it? It's on the 1001 list, so yes.) Overwhelming, I guess, in the sense that apathy is overwhelmingly sad, futile, and UN-ARTISTIC.<br /><br />With "The Comfort of Strangers" we begin to form the ugly side of the poetics of this gifted British author. When his books are interested in things, the narrative becomes vile; when there is no interest for Emotion or Depths in said objects (hell, even the vase in "Atonement" is a murky symbol of past bourgeoisie lifestyles colliding/breaking with the harsh pullings of war... but it remains a murkyish symbol nonetheless) the quality of novel really does suffer.<br /><br />"Comfort" is all about the brutal nature of strangeness-- yes, people: bad stuff happens. Even if you do have the privilege of traveling throughout European cities and dark avenues for an entire month... even then, bad stuff can happen to you.</span>
  <a data-text-id="17541601011567455382" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_3616393495" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3616393495" />
    <a title="RACA" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/63543405-raca"><img alt="RACA" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1558647331p2/63543405.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3616393495?book_show_action=true">Oct 28, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="RACA" class="user" itemprop="url" name="RACA" href="/user/show/63543405-raca">RACA</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9789755394329" href="/book/show/7628691-yabanc-kucak">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3616393495" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7434921629176569495">Şimdi, ince bir kitapta spoiler vermeden ne yazacağım bilemiyorum. Colin ve Mary tatile çıkıyorlar, yeni yer keşfediyorlar, keyifliler. Sonra bir çiftle tanışıyorlar ve işin rengi değişiyor. Ne olacak diye beklemeye başlıyorsunuz. Adım adım tahmin ettiğim son gelecek mi diye bekliyorsunuz.<br/>McEwan okumaya, onun karanlık anlatımına alışkınım aslında. Bu kitapta beklentim mi farklıydı bilmiyorum, hoşuma gitti. <br/><br/>"Aslında ona acıyan olsaydı dayanamaz yıkılırdı. Böylece içinde bulunduğu şok durumu sürmü</span>
  <span id="freeText7434921629176569495" style="display:none">Şimdi, ince bir kitapta spoiler vermeden ne yazacağım bilemiyorum. Colin ve Mary tatile çıkıyorlar, yeni yer keşfediyorlar, keyifliler. Sonra bir çiftle tanışıyorlar ve işin rengi değişiyor. Ne olacak diye beklemeye başlıyorsunuz. Adım adım tahmin ettiğim son gelecek mi diye bekliyorsunuz.<br />McEwan okumaya, onun karanlık anlatımına alışkınım aslında. Bu kitapta beklentim mi farklıydı bilmiyorum, hoşuma gitti. <br /><br />"Aslında ona acıyan olsaydı dayanamaz yıkılırdı. Böylece içinde bulunduğu şok durumu sürmüş, duyguları ondan uzaklarda bir yere gitmişti."<br /><br />🌟</span>

<div id="review_2612371504" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2612371504" />
    <a title="Maxwell" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1381728-maxwell"><img alt="Maxwell" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1539318702p2/1381728.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2612371504?book_show_action=true">Dec 03, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Maxwell" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Maxwell" href="/user/show/1381728-maxwell">Maxwell</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1381728-maxwell?shelf=short-fiction">short-fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2612371504" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1878113845805542378">[<b>4.5 stars</b>]<br/><br/>McEwan has a fixation on the intimacy between men and women, both physical and emotional, and he explores that in a fascinating way in <i>The Comfort of Strangers</i>.<br/><br/>The story follows Colin and Mary, a couple who've been together for several years but aren't married, as they spend a holiday in an unnamed city (though it's very clearly Venice—why McEwan chooses to keep this masked intrigues me). They are both quite normal, just as self-absorbed, misguided and flawed as anyone. One evening th</span>
  <span id="freeText1878113845805542378" style="display:none">[<b>4.5 stars</b>]<br /><br />McEwan has a fixation on the intimacy between men and women, both physical and emotional, and he explores that in a fascinating way in <i>The Comfort of Strangers</i>.<br /><br />The story follows Colin and Mary, a couple who've been together for several years but aren't married, as they spend a holiday in an unnamed city (though it's very clearly Venice—why McEwan chooses to keep this masked intrigues me). They are both quite normal, just as self-absorbed, misguided and flawed as anyone. One evening they get lost amongst the city's many small alleys and meet a man named Robert who takes them to a bar (his bar, which he fails to reveal until later) and eventually to meet his wife, Caroline. <br /><br />After this brief encounter, some unusual situations occur and things eventually meet quite a dramatic ending. I won't say much else because if you're like me, you will be utterly transfixed by this story. And as it's rather short, you won't want to put it down until you turn that last page.<br /><br />McEwan is a masterful storyteller. Somehow he manages to maintain brilliant writing, complex themes, well-crafted characters, and a thrilling story. It's bizarre and baffling and honestly kept me on my toes throughout. I think with time and more consideration (I've literally just finished reading it), this could be a 5-star read for me. And it gives me even more faith in picking up more of McEwan's works. </span>
  <a data-text-id="1878113845805542378" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9788806224776" href="/book/show/24844578-cortesie-per-gli-ospiti">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/34720769-amaranta?shelf=inglesi">inglesi</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2110298817" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15496228794091809179">Colin e Mary sono una coppia di turisti in vacanza. Sono indolenti, vivono le loro giornate fra le lenzuola fresche di un hotel, uscendo la sera a cena, bevendo e fumando qualcosa. Non sappiamo dove sono. Una città che dalla descrizione sembrerebbe Venezia. E ci trascinano nelle loro giornate senza senso, in discussioni silenziose in un tempo immobile, in un’abitudine ai loro corpi che è normalità, piacere, delicatezza e conforto. Ognuno si prende cura dell’altro come sa. La certezza dell’esserc</span>
  <span id="freeText15496228794091809179" style="display:none">Colin e Mary sono una coppia di turisti in vacanza. Sono indolenti, vivono le loro giornate fra le lenzuola fresche di un hotel, uscendo la sera a cena, bevendo e fumando qualcosa. Non sappiamo dove sono. Una città che dalla descrizione sembrerebbe Venezia. E ci trascinano nelle loro giornate senza senso, in discussioni silenziose in un tempo immobile, in un’abitudine ai loro corpi che è normalità, piacere, delicatezza e conforto. Ognuno si prende cura dell’altro come sa. La certezza dell’esserci li mantiene in equilibrio, un equilibrio che sembra instabile, nonostante la stasi del libro. Sono fermi e inquieti allo stesso tempo. Forse per questo si affidano ad una coppia conosciuta per caso. Le parole sono scelte con cura, nulla è in più o in meno, asciutte, tragiche, creano una tensione nell’immobilità che allerta i sensi del lettore fino alla fine, forse solo un po’ frettolosa, ma a rifletterci non avrebbe potuto essere diversa. Una lettura ottima, come nella migliore tradizione di McEwan.</span>

<div id="review_3302264841" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3302264841" />
    <a title="tunalizade" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/6675208-tunalizade"><img alt="tunalizade" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1589133266p2/6675208.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3302264841?book_show_action=true">May 01, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="tunalizade" class="user" itemprop="url" name="tunalizade" href="/user/show/6675208-tunalizade">tunalizade</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9789750846953" href="/book/show/53265942-yabanc-kucak">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3302264841" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7678444888412326987">Mary ve Colin'nin ilişkilerindeki tutku muhtemelen her çiftin sahip olmak için can attığı cinsten. İkisi de oturup düzeyli bir şekilde bir konu hakkında fikirlerini tartışabiliyor, olaylar karşısında yorum kabiliyetleri yüksek, birbirlerine olan sevgileri aşk olarak tanımlanıyor ve saatlerce sevişebiliyorlar. Aralarındaki etkileşimin sonuçları ortaya ideale yakın bir çift çıkmasına sebebiyet veriyor.<br/><br/>Bu tutkulu iki insan gittikleri tatilde şehri keşfediyor, yemekler yiyip içiyor, bolca sevişiyor.</span>
  <span id="freeText7678444888412326987" style="display:none">Mary ve Colin'nin ilişkilerindeki tutku muhtemelen her çiftin sahip olmak için can attığı cinsten. İkisi de oturup düzeyli bir şekilde bir konu hakkında fikirlerini tartışabiliyor, olaylar karşısında yorum kabiliyetleri yüksek, birbirlerine olan sevgileri aşk olarak tanımlanıyor ve saatlerce sevişebiliyorlar. Aralarındaki etkileşimin sonuçları ortaya ideale yakın bir çift çıkmasına sebebiyet veriyor.<br /><br />Bu tutkulu iki insan gittikleri tatilde şehri keşfediyor, yemekler yiyip içiyor, bolca sevişiyor. Yine bir akşam yemek için çıktıkları sokakta gezerken kayboluyor ve karşılarına çıkan Robert ile sohbet paylaşımlarında bulunuyorlar. Robert'in onlar için önerileri var ve çiftimiz bu önerilere kulak veriyor. Ardından Robert ile Mary-Colin arasında geçen fikir beyanları ile çiftimiz Robert'in evine davet ediliyor. Burada Robert'in eşi ile tanışan Mary ve Colin bu vakitten sonra başlarına gelebilecek enteresan, gergin ve bir o kadar da karanlık olayların içine çekiliyor.<br /><br />Ian McEwan'dan okuduğum üçüncü kitap Yabancı Kucak ve bu kitabı okuduğum için o kadar mutluyum ki. Sadece üç kitap sonucunda yazar için en sevdiğim yazarlar arasında diyorum. Ian McEwan ciddi anlamda kalemi muhteşem biri. Cümleleri basit tanımlamasından oldukça uzak, ifadeleri arasına serpiştirilmiş detaylar ise bir o kadar derin. Yabancı Kucak ile de bu ivmeyi yakalamış. Okumaya başladığınız andan itibaren yavaş yavaş ama enerjisi artan bir gerilimin içine sürükleniyorsunuz. Üstelik bu bir gerilim romanı mı onu bilemiyorum. Fakat bir kitabı okurken bu kadar gerçekçilik içinde gerilim hissini yaşadığımı hatırlamıyorum. Çünkü olayları anbean yaşanırken okuyor gibisiniz. <br /><br />Özellikle kitabın sonu ise benim aradığım duvara çarpılma hissini fazlasıyla veriyor. Kitabı bitirdiğimde allak bullak oluşumun başka açıklaması olamaz. Beklenmedik sona sahip bu kitabı muhtemelen soluksuz okuyacaksınız.<br /><br />Henüz okumamış olanlar için tavsiyemdir.</span>
  <a data-text-id="7678444888412326987" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_205490565" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/205490565" />
    <a title="Shovelmonkey1" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3926982-shovelmonkey1"><img alt="Shovelmonkey1" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1391713268p2/3926982.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/205490565?book_show_action=true">Sep 05, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Shovelmonkey1" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Shovelmonkey1" href="/user/show/3926982-shovelmonkey1">Shovelmonkey1</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780330268295" href="/book/show/964899.The_Comfort_of_Strangers">review of another edition</a>

          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">anyone about to go on holiday</span></div>

          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to Shovelmonkey1 by: </span>
      1001 books list
  </div>


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3926982-shovelmonkey1?shelf=1001-books">1001-books</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3926982-shovelmonkey1?shelf=bookcrossing-books">bookcrossing-books</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3926982-shovelmonkey1?shelf=murder-most-horrid">murder-most-horrid</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer205490565" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10116419767972688545">We're all going on a (pause) summer holiday. No more working for a week or two....<br/><br/>Or permanently because we make poor choices about accepting cordial invites from complete strangers. What is it about a glass of two of Prosecco on a warm summers afternoon that makes people so trusting of random strangers when they're on holiday? Perhaps we find some of our holiday locations so idealistic and romantic that it's like a fairy tale and so we'll merrily gad about, without a care in the world, believin</span>
  <span id="freeText10116419767972688545" style="display:none">We're all going on a (pause) summer holiday. No more working for a week or two....<br /><br />Or permanently because we make poor choices about accepting cordial invites from complete strangers. What is it about a glass of two of Prosecco on a warm summers afternoon that makes people so trusting of random strangers when they're on holiday? Perhaps we find some of our holiday locations so idealistic and romantic that it's like a fairy tale and so we'll merrily gad about, without a care in the world, believing that no harm can befall us. Yeah right pay attention people. <br /><br />Murder on the orient express? Amity Island in Jaws? Cabin Fever? The Beach? Any of these things ringing a bell? Whether you're enjoying some quiet time by the ocean, on a cruise or simply enjoying the back woods in butt-fuck nowhere with your drunken jock friends, beware YOU ARE NEVER FAR FROM A PSYCHO!<br /><br />Ian McEwan likes to play on these kinds of fears and lets face it, with psychos aplenty roaming the highways and byways of our fair planet he is planting these little worry seeds in pretty fertile ground. The Comfort of Strangers is a warning to all overtly trusting day-trippers, euro-hounds and lovers of the city break.<br /><br />At first I didn't have much interest in the two primary characters but by Chapter 4 I was, despite myself, really enjoying this book. Initially Mary and Colin seem smug and dull. They come across as vain and their relationship appeared to be based on neediness rather than love, like two whiny puppies pawing at each other for affection and attention. However the way they interface with their holiday environment (in a nameless place but descriptions suggest Venice) is how many people have experienced a foreign city. McEwan does an excellent job of capturing the tiredness, even despair you can sometimes feel when lost in a foreign place. That exact moment when a holiday ceases to be fun and becomes more like an endurance test. Enter the new friends; welcoming and there to lend a guiding hand. Or are they? As usual McEwan provides a twist in the tale. It's his literary raison d'etre after all. Better than On Chesil Beach but not as good as the Cement Garden. </span>
  <a data-text-id="10116419767972688545" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1228921630" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7249539934676016913">Creepy and spare, this will not be among my favorite of McEwan's remarkable <i>oeuvre</i>. And yet. <i>The Comfort of Strangers</i> was published nearly thirty-five years ago and there is a timelessness about its tone and the shadowy, shuddery story. Perhaps because its setting-Venice (unnamed, but sketched down to the detail of aroma of decay and filtered sunlight)-has not changed. McEwan carefully withheld markers of politics and pop culture, as if somehow knowing the story's potential to live past its Last</span>
  <span id="freeText7249539934676016913" style="display:none">Creepy and spare, this will not be among my favorite of McEwan's remarkable <i>oeuvre</i>. And yet. <i>The Comfort of Strangers</i> was published nearly thirty-five years ago and there is a timelessness about its tone and the shadowy, shuddery story. Perhaps because its setting-Venice (unnamed, but sketched down to the detail of aroma of decay and filtered sunlight)-has not changed. McEwan carefully withheld markers of politics and pop culture, as if somehow knowing the story's potential to live past its Last-Days-of-Disco era. It's fascinating to read this early novel, McEwan's second, to see the same elegant, structured prose, but absent of the warmth and humanity he's achieved in later works. <i>The Comfort of Strangers</i> is like a highly-stylized, black-and-white <i>neo-noir</i>, where the actors say profound things that make little sense. But it's still pretty cool. <br /> </span>

          </span>

<span id="freeTextContainer7257109488056807412">This is the earliest McEwan work I’ve read (1981). I could see the seeds of his classic themes: obsession, sexual and otherwise; and the slow building of suspense and awareness until an inevitable short burst of violence. Mary and Colin are a vacationing couple in Venice. One evening they’ve spent so long in bed that by the time they get out all the local restaurants have shut, but a bar-owner takes pity and gives them sustenance, then a place to rest and wash when they get lost and fail to loca</span>
  <span id="freeText7257109488056807412" style="display:none">This is the earliest McEwan work I’ve read (1981). I could see the seeds of his classic themes: obsession, sexual and otherwise; and the slow building of suspense and awareness until an inevitable short burst of violence. Mary and Colin are a vacationing couple in Venice. One evening they’ve spent so long in bed that by the time they get out all the local restaurants have shut, but a bar-owner takes pity and gives them sustenance, then a place to rest and wash when they get lost and fail to locate their hotel. Soon neighborly solicitude turns into a creepy level of attention. McEwan has the knack for presenting situations that are just odd enough to stand out but not odd enough to provoke an instant recoil, so along with the characters we keep thinking all will turn out benignly. This reminded me of <i>Death in Venice</i> and <i>The Talented Mr. Ripley</i>.</span>

  <div class="left bodycol">

        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1370527345?book_show_action=true">Aug 21, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Sandra" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Sandra" href="/user/show/11916501-sandra">Sandra</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9788806224776" href="/book/show/24844578-cortesie-per-gli-ospiti">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/11916501-sandra?shelf=gran-bretagna">gran-bretagna</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1370527345" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13143477480189932162">Un romanzo enigmatico e inquietante, che parte lento e sonnolento, nel descrivere la relazione tra Colin e Mary, una coppia che non si parla più eppure ciascuno conosce l’altro almeno come sé stesso, per la lunga e abitudinaria convivenza, in vacanza in una città innominata, dominata dall’acqua –un’acqua stagnante ed immobile come le esistenze dei due protagonisti-. Il racconto prosegue in un’atmosfera onirica, come nel mondo ovattato del sogno fatto di silenzi e di gesti lenti e sguardi muti.<br/>Un</span>
  <span id="freeText13143477480189932162" style="display:none">Un romanzo enigmatico e inquietante, che parte lento e sonnolento, nel descrivere la relazione tra Colin e Mary, una coppia che non si parla più eppure ciascuno conosce l’altro almeno come sé stesso, per la lunga e abitudinaria convivenza, in vacanza in una città innominata, dominata dall’acqua –un’acqua stagnante ed immobile come le esistenze dei due protagonisti-. Il racconto prosegue in un’atmosfera onirica, come nel mondo ovattato del sogno fatto di silenzi e di gesti lenti e sguardi muti.<br />Un incontro con un singolare personaggio di cui sappiamo solo il nome, Robert, smuove le acque intorno a Colin e Mary, che cominciano ad annaspare nell’acqua trasformata in mare increspato dalle onde, da affrontare con estrema attenzione perché può travolgere e annegare tutto ciò che incontra. La morte aleggia sinistra come il vento che dà forza al moto marino, in un crescendo di tensione ed ansia che McEwan dosa in modo sapiente, grazie a piccoli particolari, a gesti apparentemente insignificanti, a sguardi misteriosi, a frasi spezzate, in un gioco macabro tra Colin e Mary, da un lato, e Robert con sua moglie Caroline dall’altro. Fino all’epilogo orribile, che sconvolge, anche se annunciato, perché le acque calme della coscienza sono sopraffatte dal tremendo uragano delle pulsioni umane che esplodono con violenza.<br />McEwan è uno scrittore abilissimo, riesce a tenere incollati al libro pur non desiderando altro che gettarlo via, per il disagio e l’inquietudine che ne permeano ogni riga. E poi come scrive bene, con uno stile corposo e fluido che dà vita ad una lettura in ci si gusta ogni frase ed ogni parola.<br /></span>

<div id="review_2540003054" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2540003054" />
    <a title="Konstantin" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/60906430-konstantin"><img alt="Konstantin" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1537659835p2/60906430.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2540003054?book_show_action=true">Sep 23, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Konstantin" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Konstantin" href="/user/show/60906430-konstantin">Konstantin</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2540003054" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10502878439622473058">This is the first half of <i>The Stranger</i> with slightly less philosophy - it makes characterizations through geography and weather; the oppressive heat of summer and the ways it affects the characters and their moods is somewhat of a common theme in both of these.<br/><br/>It is also like <i>The Talented Mr Ripley</i> with its American/English characters in an Italian setting, strong sexual undertones, and disquieting atmosphere. It is more concise and has a bit less backstabbing and manipulating than Ripley, but i</span>
  <span id="freeText10502878439622473058" style="display:none">This is the first half of <i>The Stranger</i> with slightly less philosophy - it makes characterizations through geography and weather; the oppressive heat of summer and the ways it affects the characters and their moods is somewhat of a common theme in both of these.<br /><br />It is also like <i>The Talented Mr Ripley</i> with its American/English characters in an Italian setting, strong sexual undertones, and disquieting atmosphere. It is more concise and has a bit less backstabbing and manipulating than Ripley, but it also uses an unexpected outburst of violence as a plot device.<br /><br />Inevitably, I had to make some comparisons to <i>Tangerine</i> as well. A foreign country, sticky, humid heat, sinister strangers, a twisted bond on the very line between friendship and hostility. It also suffers from the way too short and way too underwhelming ending syndrome, but here it doesn't really affect the novel as such - because it aimed to be a character study rather than a thriller.<br /><br />So while <i>The Comfort of Strangers</i> may contain some common and recurring themes, it is also quite distinctive. This is one of the earlier McEwan's novels (1981), but his style is already showing as very potent and rounded: both lyrical, almost poetic in its descriptions, yet containing a dark, slip-under-your-skin feeling just beneath the surface. I can see how this won't be for everyone - still, it was very much for me; just a good mixture of almost everything I like to see in a novel. Recommended.</span>
  <a data-text-id="10502878439622473058" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_3388328644" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">

    <a title="Ana Cristina Lee" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/68028114-ana-cristina-lee"><img alt="Ana Cristina Lee" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1582102021p2/68028114.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3388328644?book_show_action=true">Jun 12, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Ana Cristina Lee" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Ana Cristina Lee" href="/user/show/68028114-ana-cristina-lee">Ana Cristina Lee</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9788433966933" href="/book/show/332387.El_placer_del_viajero">review of another edition</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3388328644" class="readable"

<span id="freeTextContainer2718978812218433259">Los títulos de Ian McEwan nos suelen enviar en la dirección equivocada: en este viaje no hay placer – o más dolor que placer en todo caso – y no hablemos ya del título inglés <i>The comfort of strangers</i>, ya que los desconocidos no aportan ningún consuelo en esta historia. Más bien el propósito de la narración es crear incomodidad y una tensión que va creciendo hasta el final.<br/><br/>La cita inicial de Cesare Pavese ya nos da la clave de lo que significa el viaje aquí, la ruptura con la normalidad, el queda</span>
  <span id="freeText2718978812218433259" style="display:none">Los títulos de Ian McEwan nos suelen enviar en la dirección equivocada: en este viaje no hay placer – o más dolor que placer en todo caso – y no hablemos ya del título inglés <i>The comfort of strangers</i>, ya que los desconocidos no aportan ningún consuelo en esta historia. Más bien el propósito de la narración es crear incomodidad y una tensión que va creciendo hasta el final.<br /><br />La cita inicial de Cesare Pavese ya nos da la clave de lo que significa el viaje aquí, la ruptura con la normalidad, el quedar desprotegido ante las amenazas:<br /><br /><i>Viajar es una brutalidad. Te obliga a confiar en extraños y a perder de vista todo lo que te resulta familiar y confortable de tus amigos y tu casa. </i><br /><br />El telón de fondo es una oscura ciudad turística que no se nombra en ningún momento, pero que identificamos con Venecia – de hecho la versión cinematográfica de 1990 <i>El placer de los extraños</i> se rodó allí.<br /><br />Conocemos a una pareja que llevan años juntos, Mary y Colin, y que buscan en estas vacaciones un aliciente para su relación. Perdidos en los oscuros callejones y canales que les resultan hostiles, son rescatados por Robert, residente en la ciudad, que les convida a un bar y luego a su casa donde conocen a Caroline, su mujer. A partir de aquí se desarrolla una turbia relación entre los cuatro, con una sensación de peligro que se va incrementando ante la mirada pasiva del lector, inquieto ante el cariz que toman las vacaciones de la pareja.<br /><br />Es una novela relativamente corta, unas 150 páginas y es de las primeras del autor, no se puede decir que sea una obra redonda. Pero ciertamente da que pensar, esta exploración de las relaciones de amor y dominio que pueden desembocar en formas de sexualidad ligadas con la violencia. Son temas que el autor desarrollará de manera más elaborada en otras novelas como <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/218981.S_bado" title="Sábado by Ian McEwan" rel="nofollow noopener">Sábado</a> y <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/815309.On_Chesil_Beach" title="On Chesil Beach by Ian McEwan" rel="nofollow noopener">On Chesil Beach</a>.</span>
  <a data-text-id="2718978812218433259" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
